            function initUserCache(orbitUser) {
              var availableAttr = [
                'X-Country',
                'X-Ip_is_advertise_combined',
                'X-Ip_is_uk_combined'
              ];
              var userCache = availableAttr.reduce(function(user, curr, i) {
                if (!orbitUser[curr]) {
                  return user;
                }
                var res = Object.assign({}, user);
                res[curr] = orbitUser[curr];
                return res;
              }, {});
              if (Object.keys(userCache).length !== availableAttr.length) {
                userCache._partial = true;
              }
              return userCache;
            }

            function checkStatus(response) {
              if (response.status >= 200 && response.status < 300) {
                return response;
              } else {
                var error = new Error(response.statusText);
                error.response = response;
                throw error;
              }
            }

              return typeof XMLHttpRequest !== 'undefined' &&
                'withCredentials' in new XMLHttpRequest();
            };
    
            var getUserInfoFallback = function() {
              return window.bbcpage.loadModule(['orb/fig'])
                .then(function(orbFig) {
                  return new Promise(function(resolve, reject) {
                    orbFig.load(function(fig) {
                      var getCountryFallback = function() {
                        if (fig.geo.isUK()) {
                          return 'GB';
                        } else if (fig.geo.isEU()) {
                          return 'EU';
                        }
                      };
                      resolve({
                        'X-Country': user.country || getCountryFallback(),
                        'X-Ip_is_uk_combined': fig().uk ? 'yes' : 'no',
                        'X-Ip_is_advertise_combined': fig().ad ? 'yes' : 'no'
                      });
                    }, function () {
                      reject('Error determining country. Timeout?');
                    });
                  });
                });
            };
    
            var _userInfoRequest;
            var getUserInfo = function() {
    
              var host = window.location.host.toString().match(/bbc\.com$/) ? 'bbc.com' : 'bbc.co.uk';
              var userInfoUrl = USERINFO_URL || window.location.protocol + "//" + USERINFO_PREFIX + host + '/userinfo';
    
              if (!user._partial) {
                return Promise.resolve(user);
              }
    
              if (!_userInfoRequest) {
                if (typeof window.fetch === 'undefined' || !supportsCors()) {
                  _userInfoRequest = getUserInfoFallback();
                } else {
                  _userInfoRequest = window.fetch(userInfoUrl, { credentials: 'same-origin' })
                    .then(checkStatus)
                    .then(parseJSON)
                    .then(function(userInfo) {
                      user = userInfo;
                      return user;
                    });
                }
              }
    
              return _userInfoRequest;
            };

  margin-top: auto; }
</style>

class="WaypointsList__item WaypointsList__item--is-active"><section class="article" id="worklifearticle20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus"><div><div class="article-headline  article-headline--mobile article-headline--tablet article-headline--largeTablet"><div class="article-headline__line--short"><div class="styled-line styled-line--culture styled-line--xs-small"></div></div><div class="article-headline__collection"><div class="article-labels"> <a class="article-labels__text" target="" rel="" id="" href="/worklife/tags/covid-19">Covid-19</a></div></div><div class="article-headline__text"> <!-- -->Why grocery shelves won&#x27;t be empty for long<!-- --> </div><div class="article-share-tools"><a href="mailto:?subject=Shared from class="email-icon gelicon--mail" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" id=""><span class="screen-reader-only">Share using Email</span><div id="EmailIcon"></div></a><a class="twitter-icon gelicon--twitter" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" id=""><div id="TwitterIcon"></div><span class="screen-reader-only">Share on Twitter</span></a><a hero-image--medium-mobile hero-image--tablet"><img draggable="false" title="" srcSet="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/189_106/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg 189w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/304_171/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg 304w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/624_351/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg 624w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg 976w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1280_720/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg 1280w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1600_900/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg 1600w" sizes="(min-width: 420px) 100vw, 150vw" src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/c5/p088c5lp.jpg" alt="" id=""/></div></div><div class="article__container"><div class="article__main"><div class="article__subcontainer"><article class="article__body"><div class="article__author-unit"><div class="author-unit author-unit--smal-mobile"><div class="author-unit__container author-unit__container--desktop"><a class="author-unit__text" target="" rel="" id="">By Peter Rubinstein</a><a href="https://twitter.com/@pete_rubinstein" class="author-unit__icon" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" id=""><div class="gelicon--twitter"></div></a><span class="author-unit__date"> <!-- -->2nd April 2020<!-- --> </span></div><div class="author-unit__line"><div class="styled-line styled-line--dark-grey styled-line--large"></div></div></div></div><div class="article__body-content"><div class="article__intro"> <!-- -->While the sight of empty supermarket shelves may lead shoppers to fear food shortages, experts in the food supply chain say the system is built to endure.<!-- --> </div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="drop-capped drop-capped--worklife">I</div><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--drop-capped body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>In the earliest days of coronavirus, visiting a local supermarket felt like a bad dream to many. Stepping inside and seeing checkout lines three times their usual length, and quickly realising that you weren&rsquo;t the only one who felt it was time to stock up. Shouldering past the other shoppers toward the pasta aisle or frozen section, and turning the corner in shock to find rows and rows of empty shelves.</p>
<p>As news of Covid-19 has grabbed the world&rsquo;s attention, our grocery stores, usually bursting with every item we expect, have quickly been left bare by shoppers <a href="https://www.bbc.com/worklife/article/20200304-coronavirus-covid-19-update-why-people-are-stockpiling">panic-buying</a> toilet paper, water, rice, beans, pasta, bread and frozen foods. Images circulated online of <a href="https://www.businessinsider.com/coronavirus-panic-empty-shelves-long-lines-at-grocery-stores-2020-3#and-as-the-virus-spread-elsewhere-in-the-weeks-that-followed-shoppers-around-the-globe-started-to-react-in-the-same-way-3">empty shelves</a> in late January, leading buyers to queue up ahead of stores&rsquo; openings and run essential items dry on e-shopping sites like <a href="https://www.usatoday.com/story/tech/2020/03/15/amazon-toilet-paper-water-household-items/5055632002/">Amazon Fresh</a>. Compared to the same week in 2019, sales of US sales of <a href="https://www.nielsen.com/us/en/insights/article/2020/key-consumer-behavior-thresholds-identified-as-the-coronavirus-outbreak-evolves/">dried beans grew 37%, rice 25% and pasta 10%</a>.</p>
<p>Now, as April begins and shoppers continue to bulk-buy, grocery chains have jumped into action. Retailers have united with manufacturers, warehouse workers and supply chain operators to implement emergency policies to meet these skyrocketing demands. But even amid the uncertainty &minus; and despite the seeming scarcity &minus; experts across the food system are looking to reassure us against what could be shoppers&rsquo; ultimate fear: that an overburdened food supply chain could lead to a food shortage.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__pull-quote"><blockquote class="inline-quote inline-quote--worklife"><h2 class="simple-header simple-header--serif-light-italic simple-header--large">What a crisis like the novel coronavirus reveals about the food system, more so than its weak points, is actually its flexibility and strength under pressure</h2></blockquote></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>&ldquo;I can definitely understand people&rsquo;s concern. Whenever they go into the grocery store, they&rsquo;re used to seeing everything&hellip; but fundamentally, when you think of food production and distribution, food is produced at a high rate right now,&rdquo; says Lowell Randel, vice president of the Global Cold Chain Alliance (GCCA) in the US.</p>
<p>What a crisis like the novel coronavirus reveals about the food system, more so than its weak points, is actually its flexibility and strength under pressure. The supply chain relies on several industry-spanning mechanisms that are designed to adapt when natural disasters strike &ndash; or when food sectors need to pivot during seasonal production spikes. In other words, we&rsquo;ve been here before.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__image-text article-body__image-text--landscape"><div><img draggable="false" srcSet="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/189_106/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg 189w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/304_171/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg 304w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/624_351/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg 624w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg 976w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1280_720/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg 1280w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1600_900/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg 1600w" sizes="(min-width: 800px) 70vw, 100vw" src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/cd/p088cdjx.jpg" id=""/><div class="inline-image__description"><div class="text-summary"><p class="text-summary__text text-summary__text--grey text-summary__text--left">About four months of food inventory are stored between the factory and grocery store at any one time. (Credit: Getty Images)</p></div></div></div></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>&ldquo;This one&rsquo;s a little different because it&rsquo;s prolonged and it&rsquo;s everywhere&hellip; [but] when a hurricane is approaching the country, consumer behaviour is exactly the same [as right now],&rdquo; says Fred Boehler, CEO of US-based supply chain firm Americold Logistics. Behaviour patterns may be the same, but when food demand is amplified to unprecedented scale across entire nations, many factories must shift to &ldquo;full capacity&rdquo; &ndash; a state of maximum production rate typically saved for emergency situations like this pandemic.</p>
<p>Adjusting to shoppers&rsquo; changing demand in the wake of Covid-19 has been a herculean task, but experts agree that doing so is well within the system&rsquo;s control &ndash; and not cause for alarm.</p>
<p><strong>Squeezing the balloon</strong></p>
<p>According to Boehler, a misunderstanding about the state of the food supply chain is that it&rsquo;s currently being strained to its breaking point. To understand the mystery of the empty shelf, you must first look upstream, beginning in the storerooms of those very supermarkets.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__pull-quote"><blockquote class="inline-quote inline-quote--worklife"><h2 class="simple-header simple-header--serif-light-italic simple-header--large">At any one time, our grocery stores are carrying about 20 to 30 days of inventory on-hand in the store  Fred Boehler</h2></blockquote></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>At any one time, &ldquo;our grocery stores are carrying about 20 to 30 days of inventory on-hand in the store,&rdquo; says Boehler. The shops receive that inventory from local &ldquo;retail distribution centres&rdquo;, where workers sort through products and organise specific orders for delivery &minus; these centres also hold about 30 days of inventory. &ldquo;Call it 60 days of on-hand inventory. It&rsquo;s all owned by that retailer.&rdquo;</p>
<p>Go back another node now to the &ldquo;regional distribution centres&rdquo;, situated in main cities, which supply the local retail centres. You guessed it: another 30 days of inventory. Finally, we reach the production facilities located directly beside the factories producing and packaging the food items. They take the product hot off the line and store it for shipping out to the regional centres. Thirty more days of inventory there.</p>
<p>These four nodes of the general supply chain are therefore in perpetual ownership of about four months of food, ready and waiting to be transported, ordered, delivered and stocked. In a world clear of pandemics and disasters, the food system would normally split its inventory between retail shops (i.e. supermarkets) and food services (restaurants and bars). But as part of the emergency measures taken over the past three weeks to reduce the spread of the virus, countries like <a href="https://time.com/5801497/italy-shops-restaurants-coronavirus/">Italy</a>, <a href="https://www.restaurantbusinessonline.com/financing/france-spain-close-down-their-restaurants">Spain, France</a> and <a href="https://www.bbc.com/news/world-us-canada-51902681">parts of the US</a> have forced restaurants to close their doors.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__image-text article-body__image-text--landscape"><div><img draggable="false" srcSet="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/189_106/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg 189w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/304_171/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg 304w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/624_351/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg 624w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg 976w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1280_720/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg 1280w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1600_900/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg 1600w" sizes="(min-width: 800px) 70vw, 100vw" src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/cf/p088cfvt.jpg" id=""/><div class="inline-image__description"><div class="text-summary"><p class="text-summary__text text-summary__text--grey text-summary__text--left">Some factories have shifted to &quot;full capacity&quot;  a state of maximum production rate typically saved for emergency situations. (Credit: Getty Images)</p></div></div></div></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>&ldquo;Food services may have represented 50%, now they represent about 10%,&rdquo; says Boehler. The food that would usually be shipped to restaurants is sitting in warehouses, while retail inventory orders have shot up simultaneously. Facilities are still shipping out the same amount of food, but their orders have radically shifted toward retail. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s kind of like squeezing a balloon,&rdquo; he adds. &ldquo;Less food services, more retail.&rdquo;</p>
<p><strong>Production mechanisms</strong></p>
<p>To keep up with grocers&rsquo; requesting 20 to 25% higher volume, providers are falling back on alternative but reliable procedures designed to handle a marketplace in flux. Some production and shipping facilities have increased their operating hours to reach full capacity and churn out as much food as possible.</p>
<p>One flour mill in Hertfordshire, England hired enough staff to move from a five-day to a seven-day operation. This enabled the company to produce an extra 350,000 bags of flour a week, says Jose Arturo Garza-Reyes, head of the Centre for Supply Chain Improvement at the University of Derby.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__pull-quote"><blockquote class="inline-quote inline-quote--worklife"><h2 class="simple-header simple-header--serif-light-italic simple-header--large">Another mechanism is to cut down on the variety of different products being made. Pasta companies, for example, can halt different varieties and sizes of pasta to only produce the core types</h2></blockquote></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>Another mechanism, often used in parallel to longer operating times, is to cut down on the variety of different products being made &ndash; trading product range to focus on volume. Pasta companies, for example, can halt different varieties and sizes of pasta to only produce the core types, Garza-Reyes says. UK supermarket chain Tesco is supplying up to double its normal quantities of milk, bread, rice and pasta while simplifying its orders, such as refocusing milk production to two and four pints.</p>
<p>Manufacturers can also increase capacity by outsourcing some of their production to other companies nationally or abroad. &ldquo;This is pretty much similar to what the UK government is doing by hiring private hospitals to boost the capacity of the NHS,&rdquo; Garza-Reyes says.</p>
<p>Yet while most companies are capable of increasing production in drastic ways, for many of them, the effect of this increased food demand is a much quieter affair. Steve Gonzalez, founder of organic pasta company Sfoglini, which produces about 6,000 pounds (2,700kg) of pasta daily, has seen a significant bump in sales since shoppers began clearing shelves. He typically orders one or two truckloads of semolina flour per month to produce pasta in his New York factory, which is then sold by Whole Foods, Stop &amp; Shop and other retailers. In March, he had to order just three &ndash; a nonissue for a company like Sfoglini with the bandwidth to adapt.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__image-text article-body__image-text--landscape"><div><img draggable="false" srcSet="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/189_106/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg 189w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/304_171/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg 304w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/624_351/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg 624w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg 976w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1280_720/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg 1280w,https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/1600_900/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg 1600w" sizes="(min-width: 800px) 70vw, 100vw" src="https://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/live/976_549/images/live/p0/88/cg/p088cg9n.jpg" id=""/><div class="inline-image__description"><div class="text-summary"><p class="text-summary__text text-summary__text--grey text-summary__text--left">The &#x27;empty shelves phenomenon&#x27; will end once customers have enough products in their storerooms, at which point no more stockpiling will be needed. (Credit: Getty Images)</p></div></div></div></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>&ldquo;The shelf movement is so fast,&rdquo; Gonzalez says. Even still, &ldquo;nobody&rsquo;s freaking out. Prices are the same.&rdquo; His suppliers haven&rsquo;t reported any hiccups, and the grains used for making the flour he purchases are safely stored in silos between their biannual harvests. It only takes about two weeks from the time the flour is milled to the time Sfoglini pasta appears on shelves, he says, so his company&rsquo;s changes &ndash; while relatively minor &minus; have had to roll out quickly. &ldquo;The benefit to us of being a smaller business is we&rsquo;re not this big, overgrown, lugging dinosaur. We can pivot and adjust as we need to.&rdquo;</p>
<p>These strategies &ndash; from the nimble adjustments within smaller companies to the more sweeping overhauls within the major players &ndash; have not stoked anxiety in the food supply chain, says Randel of the GCCA. The result is a system well in control of demand, even in the midst of a pandemic.</p>
<p><strong>Workers wanted</strong></p>
<p>The true source of the empty-shelf issue lies inside the retail shops themselves. &ldquo;In other words, and in many occasions, supermarkets do actually have products in their storerooms, but they do not have enough staff to bring it to the shelves as fast as they are taken from them,&rdquo; Garza-Reyes says. In response, major retailers around the world have begun hiring new workers to fill the gaps.</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__pull-quote"><blockquote class="inline-quote inline-quote--worklife"><h2 class="simple-header simple-header--serif-light-italic simple-header--large">US-based Aldi will recruit 5,000 temporary and 4,000 permanent staff, and Albertsons plans to bring on 30,000. Tesco in the UK has announced it will be hiring 20,000 temporary workers</h2></blockquote></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>US-based <a href="https://qz.com/1823537/the-companies-with-jobs-hiring-during-the-coronavirus-crisis/">Aldi</a> will recruit 5,000 temporary and 4,000 permanent staff, and Albertsons plans to bring on 30,000. <a href="https://www.tescoplc.com/news/2020/tesco-recruit-temporary-colleagues-to-help-feed-the-nation/">Tesco</a> in the UK has announced it will be hiring 20,000 temporary workers and has increased its inventory deliveries to meet demand. Kroger, the largest supermarket retailer in the US, <a href="https://www.kroger.com/i/coronavirus-update/store-information">has reduced its operating hours</a> &ldquo;to allow more time for our employees to rest, clean and stock,&rdquo; a company spokesperson says.</p>
<p>The global network of grocery stores is thus gradually recovering from the initial system-shock of bulk-buying seen around the world, even in the countries hardest hit by the virus. &ldquo;That food supply chain is continuing to operate. You&rsquo;re not hearing of people starving in Italy,&rdquo; Randel says. &ldquo;Grocery stores have remained open and food is available.&rdquo;</p>
<p><strong>Stockpiled high</strong></p>
<p>Garza-Reyes says he expects the greater &lsquo;empty shelves phenomenon&rsquo; to be temporary not only thanks to supermarkets making their flexibility count when adapting to demand, but because &ldquo;there will be a point where customers have enough products like pasta in their storerooms that no more stockpiling will be needed.&rdquo;</p>
<p>When that point is reached, and efforts within food supply chains and grocers align to catch up with shoppers&rsquo; demands under the Covid-19 outbreak, will there emerge a &lsquo;new normal&rsquo; in the way the food system is organised?</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article-body__pull-quote"><blockquote class="inline-quote inline-quote--worklife"><h2 class="simple-header simple-header--serif-light-italic simple-header--large">People should take a deep breath, and shop for one to two weeks maximum. If you dont find what you need, come back tomorrow  Caitlin Welsh</h2></blockquote></div><div><div class="body-text-card"><div class="body-text-card__text body-text-card__text--worklife body-text-card__text--flush-text"><div><p>Likely not, according to Boehler, who says that besides a temporarily shifted workforce, the systems in place up the chain are built to withstand more crises in the future.</p>
<p>Consumers can help reduce the collective fear of food shortages by shopping as they normally would, says Caitlin Welsh, director for the Global Food Security Program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies in the US. Workers simply need time to fill those shelves.</p>
<p>&ldquo;People should take a deep breath, and shop for one to two weeks maximum,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;If you don&rsquo;t find what you need, come back tomorrow.&rdquo;</p></div></div></div></div><div class="article__end"><div class="article-end"><div class="article-end__line--long"><div class="styled-line styled-line--worklife endure.","businessUnit":"worldwide","cards":[{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EIn the earliest days of coronavirus, visiting a local supermarket felt like a bad dream to many. Stepping inside and seeing checkout lines three times their usual length, and quickly realising that you weren&rsquo;t the only one who felt it was time to stock up. Shouldering past the other shoppers toward the pasta aisle or frozen section, and turning the corner in shock to find rows and rows of empty shelves.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAs news of Covid-19 has grabbed the world&rsquo;s attention, our grocery stores, usually bursting with every item we expect, have quickly been left bare by shoppers \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Fworklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200304-coronavirus-covid-19-update-why-people-are-stockpiling\"\u003Epanic-buying\u003C\u002Fa\u003E toilet paper, water, rice, beans, pasta, bread and frozen foods. Images circulated online of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.businessinsider.com\u002Fcoronavirus-panic-empty-shelves-long-lines-at-grocery-stores-2020-3#and-as-the-virus-spread-elsewhere-in-the-weeks-that-followed-shoppers-around-the-globe-started-to-react-in-the-same-way-3\"\u003Eempty shelves\u003C\u002Fa\u003E in late January, leading buyers to queue up ahead of stores&rsquo; openings and run essential items dry on e-shopping sites like \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.usatoday.com\u002Fstory\u002Ftech\u002F2020\u002F03\u002F15\u002Famazon-toilet-paper-water-household-items\u002F5055632002\u002F\"\u003EAmazon Fresh\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Compared to the same week in 2019, sales of US sales of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.nielsen.com\u002Fus\u002Fen\u002Finsights\u002Farticle\u002F2020\u002Fkey-consumer-behavior-thresholds-identified-as-the-coronavirus-outbreak-evolves\u002F\"\u003Edried beans grew 37%, rice 25% and pasta 10%\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENow, as April begins and shoppers continue to bulk-buy, grocery chains have jumped into action. Retailers have united with manufacturers, warehouse workers and supply chain operators to implement emergency policies to meet these skyrocketing demands. But even amid the uncertainty &minus; and despite the seeming scarcity &minus; experts across the food system are looking to reassure us against what could be shoppers&rsquo; ultimate fear: that an overburdened food supply chain could lead to a food shortage.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"infographic","imageAlignment":"centre","imageOrientation":"landscape","pullQuoteImageAlignment":"centre","videoImageAlign":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-0"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"What a crisis like the novel coronavirus reveals about the food system, more so than its weak points, is actually its flexibility and strength under pressure","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-1"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;I can definitely understand people&rsquo;s concern. Whenever they go into the grocery store, they&rsquo;re used to seeing everything&hellip; but fundamentally, when you think of food production and distribution, food is produced at a high rate right now,&rdquo; says Lowell Randel, vice president of the Global Cold Chain Alliance (GCCA) in the US.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhat a crisis like the novel coronavirus reveals about the food system, more so than its weak points, is actually its flexibility and strength under pressure. The supply chain relies on several industry-spanning mechanisms that are designed to adapt when natural disasters strike &ndash; or when food sectors need to pivot during seasonal production spikes. In other words, we&rsquo;ve been here before.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088cdjx"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;This one&rsquo;s a little different because it&rsquo;s prolonged and it&rsquo;s everywhere&hellip; [but] when a hurricane is approaching the country, consumer behaviour is exactly the same [as right now],&rdquo; says Fred Boehler, CEO of US-based supply chain firm Americold Logistics. Behaviour patterns may be the same, but when food demand is amplified to unprecedented scale across entire nations, many factories must shift to &ldquo;full capacity&rdquo; &ndash; a state of maximum production rate typically saved for emergency situations like this pandemic.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAdjusting to shoppers&rsquo; changing demand in the wake of Covid-19 has been a herculean task, but experts agree that doing so is well within the system&rsquo;s control &ndash; and not cause for alarm.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESqueezing the balloon\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAccording to Boehler, a misunderstanding about the state of the food supply chain is that it&rsquo;s currently being strained to its breaking point. To understand the mystery of the empty shelf, you must first look upstream, beginning in the storerooms of those very supermarkets.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-4"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"At any one time, our grocery stores are carrying about 20 to 30 days of inventory on-hand in the store  Fred Boehler","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-5"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EAt any one time, &ldquo;our grocery stores are carrying about 20 to 30 days of inventory on-hand in the store,&rdquo; says Boehler. The shops receive that inventory from local &ldquo;retail distribution centres&rdquo;, where workers sort through products and organise specific orders for delivery &minus; these centres also hold about 30 days of inventory. &ldquo;Call it 60 days of on-hand inventory. It&rsquo;s all owned by that retailer.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EGo back another node now to the &ldquo;regional distribution centres&rdquo;, situated in main cities, which supply the local retail centres. You guessed it: another 30 days of inventory. Finally, we reach the production facilities located directly beside the factories producing and packaging the food items. They take the product hot off the line and store it for shipping out to the regional centres. Thirty more days of inventory there.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThese four nodes of the general supply chain are therefore in perpetual ownership of about four months of food, ready and waiting to be transported, ordered, delivered and stocked. In a world clear of pandemics and disasters, the food system would normally split its inventory between retail shops (i.e. supermarkets) and food services (restaurants and bars). But as part of the emergency measures taken over the past three weeks to reduce the spread of the virus, countries like \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F5801497\u002Fitaly-shops-restaurants-coronavirus\u002F\"\u003EItaly\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.restaurantbusinessonline.com\u002Ffinancing\u002Ffrance-spain-close-down-their-restaurants\"\u003ESpain, France\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bbc.com\u002Fnews\u002Fworld-us-canada-51902681\"\u003Eparts of the US\u003C\u002Fa\u003E have forced restaurants to close their doors.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-6"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088cfvt"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-7"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Food services may have represented 50%, now they represent about 10%,&rdquo; says Boehler. The food that would usually be shipped to restaurants is sitting in warehouses, while retail inventory orders have shot up simultaneously. Facilities are still shipping out the same amount of food, but their orders have radically shifted toward retail. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s kind of like squeezing a balloon,&rdquo; he adds. &ldquo;Less food services, more retail.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EProduction mechanisms\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETo keep up with grocers&rsquo; requesting 20 to 25% higher volume, providers are falling back on alternative but reliable procedures designed to handle a marketplace in flux. Some production and shipping facilities have increased their operating hours to reach full capacity and churn out as much food as possible.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne flour mill in Hertfordshire, England hired enough staff to move from a five-day to a seven-day operation. This enabled the company to produce an extra 350,000 bags of flour a week, says Jose Arturo Garza-Reyes, head of the Centre for Supply Chain Improvement at the University of Derby.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-8"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"Another mechanism is to cut down on the variety of different products being made. Pasta companies, for example, can halt different varieties and sizes of pasta to only produce the core types","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-9"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EAnother mechanism, often used in parallel to longer operating times, is to cut down on the variety of different products being made &ndash; trading product range to focus on volume. Pasta companies, for example, can halt different varieties and sizes of pasta to only produce the core types, Garza-Reyes says. UK supermarket chain Tesco is supplying up to double its normal quantities of milk, bread, rice and pasta while simplifying its orders, such as refocusing milk production to two and four pints.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EManufacturers can also increase capacity by outsourcing some of their production to other companies nationally or abroad. &ldquo;This is pretty much similar to what the UK government is doing by hiring private hospitals to boost the capacity of the NHS,&rdquo; Garza-Reyes says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EYet while most companies are capable of increasing production in drastic ways, for many of them, the effect of this increased food demand is a much quieter affair. Steve Gonzalez, founder of organic pasta company Sfoglini, which produces about 6,000 pounds (2,700kg) of pasta daily, has seen a significant bump in sales since shoppers began clearing shelves. He typically orders one or two truckloads of semolina flour per month to produce pasta in his New York factory, which is then sold by Whole Foods, Stop &amp; Shop and other retailers. In March, he had to order just three &ndash; a nonissue for a company like Sfoglini with the bandwidth to adapt.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-10"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088cg9n"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200401-covid-19-why-we-wont-run-out-of-food-during-coronavirus-11"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;The shelf movement is so fast,&rdquo; Gonzalez says. Even still, &ldquo;nobody&rsquo;s freaking out. Prices are the same.&rdquo; His suppliers haven&rsquo;t reported any hiccups, and the grains used for making the flour he purchases are safely stored in silos between their biannual harvests. It only takes about two weeks from the time the flour is milled to the time Sfoglini pasta appears on shelves, he says, so his company&rsquo;s changes &ndash; while relatively minor &minus; have had to roll out quickly. &ldquo;The benefit to us of being a smaller business is we&rsquo;re not this big, overgrown, lugging dinosaur. We can pivot and adjust as we need to.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThese strategies &ndash; from the nimble adjustments within smaller companies to the more sweeping overhauls within the major players &ndash; have not stoked anxiety in the food supply chain, says Randel of the GCCA. The result is a system well in control of demand, even in the midst of a pandemic.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EWorkers wanted\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe true source of the empty-shelf issue lies inside the retail shops themselves. &ldquo;In other words, and in many occasions, supermarkets do actually have products in their storerooms, but they do not have enough staff to bring it to the shelves as fast as they are taken from them,&rdquo; Garza-Reyes says. Cookbooks or coffee table books work well, Yara suggests. &ldquo;Angles make a big difference,&rdquo; she says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMind your skin\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn our webcam-dominated situation, practising good skincare is even more important than usual.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe camera can make bad habits &ldquo;look ten times worse,&rdquo; says Tang, as the computer screen tends to highlight things like oil on your face. She recommends blotting your face before you go live. Be on the lookout for uneven skin tone or chapped lips, too. (Tinted moisturiser is filled with subtle pigments of makeup that help your skin tone appear even.)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;It&rsquo;s not full-on foundation, but a lot of the men on camera tend to wear tinted moisturiser so it doesn&rsquo;t look like they&rsquo;re wearing just a full face of makeup,&rdquo; says Yara. is the best way to establish &ldquo;a new normal&rdquo; and avoid conflict, adds business and life coach Rebecca Morley.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EShe advises couples who are co-working from home to break up their days and weeks with shared activities, such as always having an afternoon coffee together or starting a weekend hobby. &ldquo;It means you don&rsquo;t have to keep making decisions, so it takes away a bit of the emotional load and allows you to take things one step at a time.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELundquist says it&rsquo;s also important for couples to weigh up potential exit strategies in case things go wrong. &ldquo;From a virology perspective, what we want everybody to be doing is to be as locked down as they can and staying in place. When the guest explains that he has time for other pursuits at the weekend, Maggie Smith&rsquo;s wealthy dowager \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fwatch?v=zhfpBW-nUWk\"\u003Einquires with a hint of alarm\u003C\u002Fa\u003E: &ldquo;What is a weekend?&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt&rsquo;s a question those of us with non-essential jobs have had plenty of time to ask in recent weeks as schools, offices and public spaces of all kinds have closed to slow the spread of coronavirus. Without those, people around the world have had to craft their own creative ways to distinguish downtime from regular time.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EChaney Kourouniotis is a Seattle-based marketing director for the travel company Rick Steves Europe. Though she&rsquo;s now working at home, she&rsquo;s continued to set an alarm so that she can rise and be at her desk at her regular time. Sleeping in is a pleasure she reserves for weekends.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor those with kids at home, leisurely mornings on any day are often out of the question. Emily Seftel works in administration for an international organisation in Paris, her husband works in tech, and they&rsquo;re juggling both jobs at home with care and schooling for their six-year-old son. To ensure they look forward to their weekends, they&rsquo;ve instituted a rule: each weekend day, each parent gets three hours to themselves alone, in whatever space they can find in the apartment.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;The other members of the family pretend&nbsp;that parent isn&rsquo;t there,&rdquo; Seftel says. &ldquo;We alternate mornings and afternoons. This last weekend I got Saturday morning and Sunday afternoon off to read on the balcony in the sun, lock myself in the bedroom with&nbsp;Netflix, to do whatever I wanted.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMan-made cycle\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhy do weekends matter? Unlike the 24-hour daily rotation of the Earth or its year-long journey around the Sun, the seven-day week is a purely social construct, as the journalist Katrina Onstad notes in her book The Weekend Effect: The Life-Changing Benefits of Taking Time Off and Challenging the Cult of Overwork.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn fact, the two-day weekend was in part born from another economic crisis, Onstad says. During the Great Depression of the 1930s, many industries that had not yet adopted the 40-hour workweek cut employee schedules back to five days a week, so that fewer working hours could be distributed among more people. By 1938, the 40-hour workweek was enshrined into law with the Fair Labor Standards Act.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt&rsquo;s possible that this current crisis will lead to long-standing changes as well.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Even before the coronavirus pandemic, the traditional working week was changing,&rdquo; says University of Portsmouth history professor Brad Beaven, citing the rise in remote working, self-employment and gig economy jobs. &ldquo;Strangely&nbsp;enough, self-isolation has &hellip; enabled the worker to determine their own cycle of productivity, their breaks and own work routine.&rdquo;&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-how-lockdown-redefines-our-weekends-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p088hlmg"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-how-lockdown-redefines-our-weekends-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E&lsquo;Bring some normalcy back&rsquo;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHow society will change its habits in response to this crisis remains to be seen. For individuals, though, establishing some kind of structure to the day and week is a critical component to surviving the uncertainty and strain of the coming weeks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;If you don&rsquo;t want to get things done, it doesn&rsquo;t matter&rdquo; if you have a routine, says Nir Eyal, a productivity consultant and author of Indistractable: How to Control Your Attention and Choose Your Life. &ldquo;If you need to get things done, it&rsquo;s not optional. We need structure in our day and we know that without structure, people go crazy. Literally. Rates of depression and anxiety go up [in situations] when there is low control and high expectations.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EEyal was referring to a 2006 study that showed that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F17173201\"\u003Epeople in jobs where they had little control\u003C\u002Fa\u003E of their working conditions, minimal social support and high psychological demands at work were more likely to have mental health disorders like depression and anxiety.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-how-lockdown-redefines-our-weekends-4"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"PullQuote","iFrameType":"","pullQuote":"Humans are creatures of habit, so having a regular schedule for when we work and when we engage in leisure can help us reduce uncertainty, especially in this already uncertain time  Laurie Santos","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200402-how-lockdown-redefines-our-weekends-5"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003ECompare that to our current situation, where we&rsquo;re all stuck working at home, can&rsquo;t spend time with friends, loved ones or colleagues, and are balancing the simultaneous demands of work, family and home school amid a pandemic. There&rsquo;s a lot there we can&rsquo;t control. Maintaining some kind of daily structure and sense of time helps us focus on what we can.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;I&rsquo;d encourage people to find ways to replicate what they did on weekends using the current creative things we're doing in this time. Did you do Sunday brunch every week with friends? Make some pancakes at home and meet over Zoom. Was Saturday morning the day for your big run? Then head out for a socially distanced jog,&rdquo; Santos says. &ldquo;The idea is to find ways to replicate the routines we had previously as much as possible to bring some normalcy back to the odd situation we find ourselves in.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe content of your routine isn&rsquo;t as important as the fact that you have one, Eyal says. Jim Quick, a management professor at the University of Texas at Arlington, says that (along with badly managed conflict) the main factors in occupational stress are uncertainty and a lack of control &ndash; two things which are in high supply right now.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThus, sometimes emotional eating is &ldquo;the only coping toolkit we have in our bag &ndash; especially during times like now&rdquo;, says Katherine Kimber, a registered dietitian and spokesperson for the \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.bda.uk.com\u002F\"\u003EBritish Dietetic Association\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s okay to give yourself some slack.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUnique stressors\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt might seem insensitive to talk about stress eating now, with so many people worrying about food and money due to Covid-19&rsquo;s massive economic disruption. Yet worrying about food can itself transition into emotional eating. Kimber explains that &ldquo;restriction, whether it be a physical restriction (not physically being able to access food) or an emotional restriction (perceiving foods as bad), can have a backlash effect and increase disordered eating behaviours&rdquo;.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis sense of restriction, if people are experiencing it, will now be interacting with other unique stressors linked to social isolation and sheltering in place. One of these stressors is the loss of routine for the many people whose work and social lives have been overhauled. For instance, an office worker whose weekday is partly structured around a cold meal before commuting, a purchased meal midway through the workday and a hot meal after returning home has lost that familiar way of ordering the day. Having to expend more mental energy on each of these decisions, from the timing to the possible shortages, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.headspace.com\u002Fblog\u002F2016\u002F08\u002F22\u002Fthe-secret-benefit-of-routines-it-wont-surprise-you\u002F\"\u003Ecan lead to higher anxiety\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200331-how-to-eat-a-healthy-diet-when-work-from-home-coronavirus-2"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p0888syd"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200331-how-to-eat-a-healthy-diet-when-work-from-home-coronavirus-3"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EAnother stressor at the moment is increased boredom, which is \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov\u002Fpubmed\u002F25903253\"\u003Elinked with emotional eating\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. At the same time, people have become untethered from many of their usual coping strategies, such as meeting up with friends and spending time in nature.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECaroline Kamau, an organisational psychologist at Birkbeck, University of London, whose \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fbmjopen.bmj.com\u002Fcontent\u002F9\u002F5\u002Fe027362\"\u003Eresearch\u003C\u002Fa\u003E links burnout to binge eating, points to five risk factors that might now make someone especially prone to this common form of disordered eating:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E1)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Mental health problems, especially anxiety and depression\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E2)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Body image issues, including frequent dieting and weight changes pre-pandemic\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E3)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; A highly impulsive personality, which might take the form of excessive gaming, gambling or drug use\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E4)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Emotional eating, for instance reaching for food when upset\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E5)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Friends and relatives who have disordered eating\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Most people probably binge eat once in a while but wouldn&rsquo;t be classified as having binge-eating disorder,&rdquo; Kamau is careful to point out. For instance, it&rsquo;s common to devour a whole pizza once in a while, and this wouldn&rsquo;t be concerning. Yet a milder form of this may be becoming more frequent now, even if it doesn&rsquo;t reach the threshold of a disorder. &ldquo;When you&rsquo;re in a situation where you&rsquo;re highly stressed, you&rsquo;re more prone to engage in disinhibited eating,&rdquo; Warren says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhile over-eating can feel good in the short term and provide initial comfort, this doesn&rsquo;t last. Though it was published in 2014, and specifically mentions Ebola, many of the recommendations remain relevant for Covid-19.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EA key suggestion is finding social connection. In my WhatsApp group of fellow vegans, we&rsquo;ve been sharing photos of our &ldquo;corona cupboards&rdquo; and some of our meals. It&rsquo;s a way to practise photography and exchange practical tips, but also to continue connecting by means of food &ndash; the very thing that brought us together in the first place. This could involve, for example, sharing photos of cake for people who can&rsquo;t connect in person, cooking the same favourite meal from different corners of the world, or gifting restaurant vouchers to be used in a post-coronavirus world.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOn a daily basis, Kamau emphasises the importance of structure as a way of boosting wellbeing. &ldquo;It is important for people to carry on with having aroutine, to try to wake up at set times and go to sleep at certain times,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;People shouldn&rsquo;t be tempted to sort of have a chaotic lifestyle because of the freedom that working from home comes with.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERoutines should incorporate sleep, exercise, socialising, meditation\u002Fprayer\u002Ftherapy (for people who practise them), and of course food. It&rsquo;s obvious that a healthy diet should include plenty of fruit and vegetables, but there are other rules of thumb for nourishing our psyches.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Evidence suggests that working from home can improve what we call synchronicity in family eating: having mealtimes together,&rdquo; Kamau says. Kimber acknowledges &ldquo;the huge amount of class privilege that comes with being able to follow specific dietary advice, and even more so during these uncertain times. Some people can&rsquo;t even eat consistently, because they can&rsquo;t afford it.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMore generally, Warren advises people to lean into their emotions rather than stuffing them down by means of food. Of the Covid-19 world, she says: &ldquo;This is a &lsquo;crisis situation&rsquo; that is unlikely to end soon. We&rsquo;ve got to develop some longer-term coping strategies for dealing with it.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EManage your triggers\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOne tool could be \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.cci.health.wa.gov.au\u002F~\u002Fmedia\u002FCCI\u002FMental%20Health%20Professionals\u002FGeneralised%20Anxiety\u002FGeneralised%20Anxiety%20-%20Information%20Sheets\u002FGeneralised%20Anxiety%20Information%20Sheet%20-%2005%20-%20Postpone%20your%20Worry.pdf\"\u003E&ldquo;worry time&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, where you take up to 30 minutes to bring up all the negative emotions you can about the topic that&rsquo;s worrying you. You allow yourself this period to feel your emotions as intensely as you can &ndash; screaming, writing in a diary, calling a friend or doing whatever else is necessary. Afterwards, as Warren describes it, &ldquo;you just put it to the side until your next scheduled worry time, because you really can&rsquo;t potentially sustain that amount of fear and pain and negative emotion for a whole day&rdquo;. This technique won&rsquo;t work for everyone, but Warren encourages experimentation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAnother tool is an exercise Kimber uses with her clients to find alternatives to emotional eating. This involves writing down:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Efive people you can call when you feel down (e.g. a friend)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Efive ways you can relax (e.g. take a bath)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Efive places you can go to calm down (e.g. to a cosy corner)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Efive things you can say to yourself (e.g. &ldquo;this feeling will pass&rdquo;)&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Efive activities you can use for distraction (e.g. start a puzzle)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe aim is to understand what&rsquo;s driving your craving for a chocolate bar, for example. Your body is asking you for something. So this is a good time to ask yourself, what is that?&rdquo; Kimber explains.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERelated to this, it&rsquo;s important to know our triggers. But in a crisis like this, burnout can emerge because of something different &ndash; what experts call &lsquo;decision fatigue&rsquo;.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWith news changing all the time, &ldquo;information is constant: whether it&rsquo;s about the pandemic itself, whether it&rsquo;s about things you should be doing, schedules for your kids, how to work best from home &ndash; it&rsquo;s just a lot of information&rdquo;, says Janna Koretz, a Boston-based psychologist who \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fazimuthpsych.com\u002Fteam\u002Fjanna\"\u003Ehelps clients with high-powered jobs navigate burnout\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn other words, the Covid-19 pandemic is forcing us to rapidly grapple with difficult decisions in an unsettlingly new context.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&ldquo;Normally, we&rsquo;re not thinking about \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.wired.com\u002Fstory\u002Fcoronavirus-food-delivery-gig-economy\u002F\"\u003Ethe ethical implications of ordering delivery\u003C\u002Fa\u003E or \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.wgbh.org\u002Fnews\u002Flocal-news\u002F2020\u002F03\u002F18\u002Fwant-to-help-out-your-neighbors-during-coronavirus-here-are-the-dos-and-donts\"\u003Ewhether to offer to get groceries for your elderly neighbour\u003C\u002Fa\u003E,&rdquo; says Elizabeth Yuko, a New York-based journalist and Fordham University adjunct professor in bioethics, the field that studies ethical implications in healthcare, medicine and other areas of life science. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s this psychological stress most of us aren&rsquo;t used to. Even so, this number &ndash; a rise of 3,001,000 from the previous week&rsquo;s level of 282,000 &ndash; understates the extent of the problem; \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.washingtonpost.com\u002Fbusiness\u002F2020\u002F03\u002F19\u002Funemployment-insurance-today-coronavirus\u002F\"\u003Eit doesn&rsquo;t include gig workers, the self-employed and other freelancers\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, who aren&rsquo;t eligible for unemployment insurance in many states.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAround the world economies are all experiencing similar symptoms as millions of people are laid off because of Covid-19. &ldquo;This is going to be a global pandemic of unemployment,&rdquo; says David Blustein, a professor of counselling psychology at Boston College and the author of The Importance of Work in an Age of Uncertainty: The Eroding Work Experience in America. &ldquo;I call it a crisis within a crisis.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFurther compounding these dramatic figures is the speed at which many suddenly found themselves without work &ndash; either laid off with some form of termination payment, asked to take indefinite unpaid leave or outright fired. A \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fjournals.sagepub.com\u002Fdoi\u002Fabs\u002F10.1177\u002F0891243219869381?journalCode=gasa\"\u003Enew study\u003C\u002Fa\u003E drawing on data from 100 interviews from the recently unemployed tracked participants&rsquo; feelings 12 weeks after job loss and then again a year later. &ldquo;At first people are really angry,&rdquo; says study author Sarah Damaske, an associate professor of sociology,&nbsp;labour and employment relations at Pennsylvania State University. She notes, however, her study looked at people who lost their jobs during a time of abundance, prior to this crisis.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWill there be similar anger this time around due to the exceptional circumstances we&rsquo;re living in? We can&rsquo;t predict for sure, she says, but based on her research it seems likely. &ldquo;People who get laid off feel fairly angry about it and upset with their employers,&rdquo; she notes. &ldquo;I argue that a lot of the anger comes either from feeling like you are replaceable &ndash; they found someone who was cheaper to do the work. Or, like you weren't really part of the team after all &ndash; [they] moved the work away from the team or took people off a team they had thought themselves to be a critical part of.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhat&rsquo;s more? So how can people suffering now maintain some measure of equilibrium in this unprecedented situation?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBenson suggests that in general, after any loss, people should recognise the elements of their situation they can and cannot control &ndash; and focus on the former. Identifying immediate problems (like the need to reduce household expenditure for a period) and putting some fixes in place will help, along with acknowledging that &ndash; in the short term &ndash; things will be tough and changes will be needed until the situation turns around.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E"},"cardType":"Body","iFrameType":"","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200327-unemployment-during-coronavirus-the-psychology-of-job-loss-6"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[]},"cardType":"Image","iFrameType":"","image":["p087xyy0"],"imageAlignment":"centre","id":"worklife\u002Farticle\u002F20200327-unemployment-during-coronavirus-the-psychology-of-job-loss-7"},{"bodyHtml":{"assets":[],"html":"\u003Cp\u003EPerhaps what is different about the current unemployment situation is the belief that it&rsquo;s temporary and that once things are under control, people will return to work. For someone like James Bell, this is a stabilising thought. &ldquo;I anticipate that when all is said and done, there&rsquo;s going to be a huge boom and people will want to return to the bars and restaurants, and tip big,&rdquo; he says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFurthermore, there&rsquo;s evidence that the collective nature of this experience is forging a sense that we&rsquo;re all in this together. Benson mentions that in his Manhattan neighbourhood restaurant owners are being sensitive and generous, paying people ahead or setting up campaigns to raise money for workers and support groups for families. While this won&rsquo;t solve the underlying social and economic problems, it may help people feel that they&rsquo;re not to blame for their plight. &ldquo;It is supportive of the idea that when they are feeling sad, angry, they might negate these feelings because they know they didn't lose their job because of something that they did wrong and that it isn't their fault,&rdquo; says Benson.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E&lsquo;Long haul&rsquo;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor Donna Bertaccini, the way forward is about embracing the current reality, however scary it may be. All her contract work fizzled as people retrenched, and it&rsquo;s taken a long time to rebuild. &ldquo;We were just seeing the light at the end of the tunnel in December,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But this coronavirus is a game changer.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EProjects are all on hold for the time being, yet Bertaccini remains optimistic that the current situation will improve and that work that has gone away will return in time. &ldquo;I think on some level everybody is in this boat together,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;It doesn&rsquo;t feel so isolating.&rdquo;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPart of her strategy to keep anxiety at bay is to stay informed about what resources are out there. src="https://nav.files.bbci.co.uk/orbit/1b2e292884201dd13064a9204e177864/js/orb.min.js"></script> <script type="text/javascript">require(['orb/lib/_$'], function($) {
        $.ready(function() {
            initialiseFooter();
        });

        var scrollCheckTimer;
        var didScroll;
        var previousPosition = 0;
        var delta = 5;
        var PROMO_SHOW_BREAKPOINT = 1500;
        var pageLength = 0;

        function initialiseFooter() {
            // Suppress this feature on browsers we no longer test
            var myNav = navigator.userAgent.toLowerCase();
            var oldIE = (myNav.indexOf('msie') !== -1) && parseInt(myNav.split('msie')[1], 10) <= 10;
            if (!oldIE) {
                require(['orb/cookies'], function (bbccookies) {
                    var disabledByCookie = (parseInt(bbccookies.get('ckns_nav_promofail')) || 0) >= 3;
                    if (!disabledByCookie) {
                        window.bbcuser.isUKCombined().then(function(isUK) {
                            if (isUK) {
                                setUpLazyLoading();
                            }
                        });
                    }
                });
            }
        }

        function setUpLazyLoading() {
            // offsetHeight is expensive to calculate, only do it once.
            pageLength = Math.abs(document.body.offsetHeight);

            if (pageLength < getViewportHeight() + 300 || pageLength - getCurrentPosition() < PROMO_SHOW_BREAKPOINT) {
                loadPromo();

            } else {
                window.onscroll = function () {
                    didScroll = true;
                };

                scrollCheckTimer = setInterval(function () {
                    if (didScroll) {
                        hasScrolled();
                        didScroll = false;
                    }
                }, 250);
            }
        }

        function loadPromo() {
            clearInterval(scrollCheckTimer);

            require(['orb/async/_footerpromo'], function(promo) {
                promo.init('https://navpromo.api.bbci.co.uk', '');
            });
        }

        function getViewportHeight() {
            return window.innerHeight;
        }

        function getCurrentPosition() {
            return Math.abs(window.scrollY || document.body.scrollTop || document.documentElement.scrollTop);
        }

        function hasScrolled() {
            var currentPosition = getCurrentPosition();
            if (Math.abs(previousPosition - currentPosition) <= delta) {
                return;
            }
            if (currentPosition > previousPosition) {
                if ((pageLength - currentPosition) <= PROMO_SHOW_BREAKPOINT) {
                    loadPromo();
                }
            }
            previousPosition = currentPosition;
        }
